### [304] [A Schrödinger Eigenfunction Method for Long-Horizon Stochastic Optimal Control](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.23173)
*Louis Claeys,Artur Goldman,Zebang Shen,Niao He*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Spectral reformulation of high-dimensional stochastic optimal control (SOC) with gradient drift: the HJB PDE reduces to a linear operator L unitarily equivalent to a Schrödinger operator S = -Δ + V, enabling long-horizon control via eigensystems. Analytic solution exists for symmetric LQR via the quantum harmonic oscillator, and in general the eigensystem can be learned with neural networks using a new loss to avoid reweighting issues. Empirically, the approach yields order-of-magnitude accuracy gains and reduces memory/runtime from O(Td) to O(d).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Overcome the curse of horizon length in high-dimensional SOC by exploiting a subclass where the uncontrolled drift is a gradient of a potential, which linearizes the HJB equation and admits a spectral solution.

Method: Show unitary equivalence between the linearized operator L and a Schrödinger operator S = -Δ + V; exploit spectral decomposition to describe long-horizon control. For symmetric LQR, identify S with the Hamiltonian of a quantum harmonic oscillator, yielding closed-form eigenfunctions/eigenvalues and an exact solution for arbitrary terminal cost. For general cases, train neural networks to learn the eigensystem of L, addressing reweighting issues in standard eigenfunction losses with a proposed improved loss.

Result: Analytic closed-form solution for symmetric LQR with any terminal cost. In general settings, learned eigensystem enables scalable control; empirical results show an order-of-magnitude boost in control accuracy and significant memory/runtime savings (from O(Td) to O(d)).

Conclusion: Spectral, unitary-equivalence approach provides efficient, scalable long-horizon SOC for gradient-drift problems; analytic solutions exist for special cases, while neural eigensolvers extend to broader problems with improved training losses.

Abstract: High-dimensional stochastic optimal control (SOC) becomes harder with longer planning horizons: existing methods scale linearly in the horizon $T$, with performance often deteriorating exponentially. We overcome these limitations for a subclass of linearly-solvable SOC problems-those whose uncontrolled drift is the gradient of a potential. In this setting, the Hamilton-Jacobi-Bellman equation reduces to a linear PDE governed by an operator $\mathcal{L}$. We prove that, under the gradient drift assumption, $\mathcal{L}$ is unitarily equivalent to a Schrödinger operator $\mathcal{S} = -Δ+ \mathcal{V}$ with purely discrete spectrum, allowing the long-horizon control to be efficiently described via the eigensystem of $\mathcal{L}$. This connection provides two key results: first, for a symmetric linear-quadratic regulator (LQR), $\mathcal{S}$ matches the Hamiltonian of a quantum harmonic oscillator, whose closed-form eigensystem yields an analytic solution to the symmetric LQR with \emph{arbitrary} terminal cost. Second, in a more general setting, we learn the eigensystem of $\mathcal{L}$ using neural networks. We identify implicit reweighting issues with existing eigenfunction learning losses that degrade performance in control tasks, and propose a novel loss function to mitigate this. We evaluate our method on several long-horizon benchmarks, achieving an order-of-magnitude improvement in control accuracy compared to state-of-the-art methods, while reducing memory usage and runtime complexity from $\mathcal{O}(Td)$ to $\mathcal{O}(d)$.

</details>

Method: Proposes a new sparse packing format and GPU CUDA kernels to enable efficient sparse computation in LLM feedforward layers; uses L1 regularization to induce high sparsity (over 99%) with minimal impact on downstream performance; integrates with optimized inference/training pipelines.

Result: Quantitative study shows that simple L1 regularization can yield >99% sparsity with negligible downstream performance impact; when used with the proposed kernels, sparsity yields substantial gains in throughput, energy efficiency, and memory usage that scale with model size.

Conclusion: Open-source release of code and kernels to promote adoption; sparsity is presented as a practical axis for improving efficiency and scalability of modern foundation models.

Abstract: Scaling autoregressive large language models (LLMs) has driven unprecedented progress but comes with vast computational costs. In this work, we tackle these costs by leveraging unstructured sparsity within an LLM's feedforward layers, the components accounting for most of the model parameters and execution FLOPs. To achieve this, we introduce a new sparse packing format and a set of CUDA kernels designed to seamlessly integrate with the optimized execution pipelines of modern GPUs, enabling efficient sparse computation during LLM inference and training. To substantiate our gains, we provide a quantitative study of LLM sparsity, demonstrating that simple L1 regularization can induce over 99% sparsity with negligible impact on downstream performance. When paired with our kernels, we show that these sparsity levels translate into substantial throughput, energy efficiency, and memory usage benefits that increase with model scale. We will release all code and kernels under an open-source license to promote adoption and accelerate research toward establishing sparsity as a practical axis for improving the efficiency and scalability of modern foundation models.

If one relaxes the learning objective such that the group on which we are evaluated is chosen obliviously of the sample, then our algorithm achieves the optimal $1/n$ convergence rate under group-realizability.

It records the immediate closure consequences of admissibility, develops a structural obstruction argument for faithful fixed-ontology reduction in genuinely multi-regime settings, and introduces a protected-stability template together with explicit numerical and symbolic witnesses on controlled subclasses, including convex and deductive settings. It also establishes theorem-layer results on evaluator factorization, morphisms, composition, and partial kernel-level alignment across semantically commensurable layers.

The symbolic component is deliberately restricted in scope: the paper establishes a first kernel-level compatibility result together with a controlled monotonic deductive witness. ### [308] [GEM: Guided Expectation-Maximization for Behavior-Normalized Candidate Action Selection in Offline RL](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.23232)
*Haoyu Wang,Jingcheng Wang,Shunyu Wu,Xinwei Xiao*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Offline RL often yields multimodal or branched action landscapes; unimodal policies can produce in-between actions poorly supported by data, reducing decision reliability at deployment.

Method: Train a GMM actor with critic-guided, advantage-weighted EM-style updates that preserve components while shifting mass toward high-value regions; learn a GMM behavior model to quantify support; during inference, generate a candidate set and rerank using a conservative ensemble lower confidence bound and behavior-normalized support (log-likelihood standardized within the candidate set).

Result: GEM is competitive on D4RL benchmarks and offers a simple inference-time knob (candidate count) to trade compute for decision quality without retraining.

Conclusion: GEM provides a practical framework for multimodal, controllable action selection in offline RL, combining multimodal policy learning with a tractable behavior model and a stable, budgetable inference procedure.

Abstract: Offline reinforcement learning (RL) can fit strong value functions from fixed datasets, yet reliable deployment still hinges on the action selection interface used to query them. When the dataset induces a branched or multimodal action landscape, unimodal policy extraction can blur competing hypotheses and yield "in-between" actions that are weakly supported by data, making decisions brittle even with a strong critic. We introduce GEM (Guided Expectation-Maximization), an analytical framework that makes action selection both multimodal and explicitly controllable. GEM trains a Gaussian Mixture Model (GMM) actor via critic-guided, advantage-weighted EM-style updates that preserve distinct components while shifting probability mass toward high-value regions, and learns a tractable GMM behavior model to quantify support. During inference, GEM performs candidate-based selection: it generates a parallel candidate set and reranks actions using a conservative ensemble lower-confidence bound together with behavior-normalized support, where the behavior log-likelihood is standardized within each state's candidate set to yield stable, comparable control across states and candidate budgets. Empirically, GEM is competitive across D4RL benchmarks, and offers a simple inference-time budget knob (candidate count) that trades compute for decision quality without retraining.

A sampling protocol uses an energy-based switch to bridge: (i) gradient-guided transport to high-probability regions and (ii) a mixing regime for exploration of the learned graph distribution.

Result: On molecular graph benchmarks, GEM matches or exceeds strong discrete diffusion baselines. The model’s explicit likelihood enables targeted exploration at inference time, enabling compositional generation, property-constrained sampling, and geodesic interpolation between graphs.

Conclusion: GEM closes the fidelity gap for discrete graph generative modeling by combining transport-inspired energy learning with a practical sampling switch, delivering high-quality samples and enabling controllable, constraint-aware inference.

Abstract: Energy-based models for discrete domains, such as graphs, explicitly capture relative likelihoods, naturally enabling composable probabilistic inference tasks like conditional generation or enforcing constraints at test-time. However, discrete energy-based models typically struggle with efficient and high-quality sampling, as off-support regions often contain spurious local minima, trapping samplers and causing training instabilities. This has historically resulted in a fidelity gap relative to discrete diffusion models. We introduce Graph Energy Matching (GEM), a generative framework for graphs that closes this fidelity gap. Motivated by the transport map optimization perspective of the Jordan-Kinderlehrer-Otto (JKO) scheme, GEM learns a permutation-invariant potential energy that simultaneously provides transport-aligned guidance from noise toward data and refines samples within regions of high data likelihood. Further, we introduce a sampling protocol that leverages an energy-based switch to seamlessly bridge: (i) rapid, gradient-guided transport toward high-probability regions to (ii) a mixing regime for exploration of the learned graph distribution. On molecular graph benchmarks, GEM matches or exceeds strong discrete diffusion baselines. Beyond sample quality, explicit modeling of relative likelihood enables targeted exploration at inference time, facilitating compositional generation, property-constrained sampling, and geodesic interpolation between graphs.

</details>


### [319] [SortedRL: Accelerating RL Training for LLMs through Online Length-Aware Scheduling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.23414)
*Yiqi Zhang,Huiqiang Jiang,Xufang Luo,Zhihe Yang,Chengruidong Zhang,Yifei Shen,Dongsheng Li,Yuqing Yang,Lili Qiu,Yang You*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: SortedRL introduces an online length-aware rollout scheduling strategy for RL fine-tuning of LLMs, reordering rollout samples by output length to enable large, flexible update batches with stable training, and using a cache-based mechanism to modulate off-policy influence.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RL training time is dominated by the rollout phase (up to ~70% for long trajectories) due to slow autoregressive generation and synchronization; improving rollout efficiency can substantially accelerate RL with LLMs while preserving performance.

Method: An online scheduler that reorders rollout samples by output length, prioritizing short samples to form groups for early updates, enabling large rollout batches and near on-policy micro-curricula. Adds a cache-based mechanism to control off-policy training, and an RL infrastructure with a stateful controller and rollout buffer to manage rollout and updates.

Result: Empirical evaluation on LLaMA-3.1-8B and Qwen-2.5-32B across logical puzzles and math tasks (AIME 24, Math 500, Minerval) shows SortedRL reduces rollout-related training bubble by over 50% and achieves 3.9%–18.4% higher performance than baselines given the same data.

Conclusion: SortedRL effectively mitigates rollout bottlenecks in RL for LLMs, improving both training efficiency and downstream performance, and demonstrates scalability across diverse models and tasks.

Abstract: Scaling reinforcement learning (RL) has shown strong promise for enhancing the reasoning abilities of large language models (LLMs), particularly in tasks requiring long chain-of-thought generation. However, RL training efficiency is often bottlenecked by the rollout phase, which can account for up to 70% of total training time when generating long trajectories (e.g., 16k tokens), due to slow autoregressive generation and synchronization overhead between rollout and policy updates. We propose SortedRL, an online length-aware scheduling strategy designed to address this bottleneck by improving rollout efficiency and maintaining training stability. SortedRL reorders rollout samples based on output lengths, prioritizing short samples forming groups for early updates. This enables large rollout batches, flexible update batches, and near on-policy micro-curriculum construction simultaneously. To further accelerate the pipeline, SortedRL incorporates a mechanism to control the degree of off-policy training through a cache-based mechanism, and is supported by a dedicated RL infrastructure that manages rollout and update via a stateful controller and rollout buffer. Experiments using LLaMA-3.1-8B and Qwen-2.5-32B on diverse tasks, including logical puzzles, and math challenges like AIME 24, Math 500, and Minerval, show that SortedRL reduces RL training bubble ratios by over 50%, while attaining 3.9% to 18.4% superior performance over baseline given same amount of data.

### [320] [Similarity-Aware Mixture-of-Experts for Data-Efficient Continual Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.23436)
*Connor Mclaughlin,Nigel Lee,Lili Su*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Adaptive mixture-of-experts over pre-trained models for continual learning with limited, potentially overlapping tasks, using incremental global pooling and instance-wise prompt masking to improve sample efficiency and suppress negative transfer.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing continual learning (CL) approaches often assume sufficient data per task or non-overlapping tasks; in real-world settings tasks can be small and overlap arbitrarily, causing poor transfer and interference.

Method: An adaptive mixture-of-experts (MoE) framework over pre-trained models, with two components: (1) incremental global pooling that gradually introduces prompts to reduce association noise, and (2) instance-wise prompt masking that partitions each task sample into in-distribution (aligned with current prompts) and out-of-distribution (requiring new prompts), enabling selective prompt reuse and growth.

Result: Experiments across varying data volumes and inter-task similarity show improved sample efficiency and broad applicability of the method, indicating effective handling of data scarcity and task overlap.

Conclusion: The proposed similarity-aware MoE approach mitigates negative transfer in scarce-data, overlapping-task continual learning and extends the applicability of prompt-based MoE methods to more realistic settings.

Abstract: Machine learning models often need to adapt to new data after deployment due to structured or unstructured real-world dynamics. The Continual Learning (CL) framework enables continuous model adaptation, but most existing approaches either assume each task contains sufficiently many data samples or that the learning tasks are non-overlapping. In this paper, we address the more general setting where each task may have a limited dataset, and tasks may overlap in an arbitrary manner without a priori knowledge. This general setting is substantially more challenging for two reasons. On the one hand, data scarcity necessitates effective contextualization of general knowledge and efficient knowledge transfer across tasks. On the other hand, unstructured task overlapping can easily result in negative knowledge transfer. To address the above challenges, we propose an adaptive mixture-of-experts (MoE) framework over pre-trained models that progressively establishes similarity awareness among tasks. Our design contains two innovative algorithmic components: incremental global pooling and instance-wise prompt masking. The former mitigates prompt association noise through gradual prompt introduction over time. The latter decomposes incoming task samples into those aligning with current prompts (in-distribution) and those requiring new prompts (out-of-distribution). Together, our design strategically leverages potential task overlaps while actively preventing negative mutual interference in the presence of per-task data scarcity. Experiments across varying data volumes and inter-task similarity show that our method enhances sample efficiency and is broadly applicable.

</details>


### [321] [End-to-End Efficient RL for Linear Bellman Complete MDPs with Deterministic Transitions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.23461)
*Zakaria Mhammedi,Alexander Rakhlin,Nneka Okolo*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Efficient RL in linear Bellman complete MDPs with deterministic transitions: end-to-end polynomial-time algorithm for finite action spaces and an argmax-oracle-based approach for large/infinite action spaces, achieving ε-optimal policy with polynomial dependence on horizon, feature dimension, and 1/ε.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Bridges the computational gap in linear Bellman complete RL by removing small-action-space and strong-oracle restrictions; provides a scalable solution for MDPs with stochastic rewards/initial states and deterministic dynamics.

Method: Introduces a computationally efficient algorithm that exploits linear Bellman completeness. For finite action spaces, the method is end-to-end efficient; for large/infinite action spaces, it relies on a standard argmax oracle over actions. The algorithm guarantees ε-optimal policy with polynomial sample and time complexity in horizon H, feature dimension d, and 1/ε.

Result: The algorithm achieves an ε-optimal policy with polynomial time and sample complexity in H, d, and 1/ε under deterministic transitions and stochastic initial states/rewards; finite action spaces are fully efficient, while large action spaces require only an argmax oracle.

Conclusion: Demonstrates that efficient learning is feasible in the linear Bellman complete setting with deterministic dynamics, expanding applicability and reducing oracle requirements. Points to future work in relaxing determinism, exploring other function classes, or tightening complexity constants.

Abstract: We study reinforcement learning (RL) with linear function approximation in Markov Decision Processes (MDPs) satisfying \emph{linear Bellman completeness} -- a fundamental setting where the Bellman backup of any linear value function remains linear. While statistically tractable, prior computationally efficient algorithms are either limited to small action spaces or require strong oracle assumptions over the feature space. We provide a computationally efficient algorithm for linear Bellman complete MDPs with \emph{deterministic transitions}, stochastic initial states, and stochastic rewards. For finite action spaces, our algorithm is end-to-end efficient; for large or infinite action spaces, we require only a standard argmax oracle over actions. Our algorithm learns an $\varepsilon$-optimal policy with sample and computational complexity polynomial in the horizon, feature dimension, and $1/\varepsilon$.

### [322] [Byzantine-Robust and Differentially Private Federated Optimization under Weaker Assumptions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.23472)
*Rustem Islamov,Grigory Malinovsky,Alexander Gaponov,Aurelien Lucchi,Peter Richtárik,Eduard Gorbunov*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Proposes Byz-Clip21-SGD2M, a federated learning algorithm that combines robust aggregation with double momentum and gradient clipping to achieve Byzantine robustness and differential privacy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Tackling the co-occurring challenges of privacy leakage and byzantine attacks in FL without relying on unrealistic assumptions (e.g., bounded gradients) or extra data, by providing convergence guarantees under standard assumptions.

Method: Introduces Byz-Clip21-SGD2M, integrating robust aggregation with double momentum and carefully designed clipping. The analysis yields high-probability convergence under L-smoothness and σ-sub-Gaussian gradient noise, relaxing prior conditions; the approach recovers optimal rates without adversaries and improves utility under Byzantine and DP settings. Empirical validation uses CNN and MLP on MNIST.

Result: The paper proves high-probability convergence guarantees for Byz-Clip21-SGD2M under standard smoothness and sub-Gaussian gradient noise assumptions, matching state-of-the-art rates in non-adversarial cases and providing improved utility bounds under Byzantine and DP settings; empirical results on MNIST corroborate effectiveness.

Conclusion: Byz-Clip21-SGD2M offers a unified solution to FL privacy and robustness, delivering rigorous convergence guarantees and practical performance in Byzantine and DP scenarios, with empirical validation on common architectures and datasets.

Abstract: Federated Learning (FL) enables heterogeneous clients to collaboratively train a shared model without centralizing their raw data, offering an inherent level of privacy. However, gradients and model updates can still leak sensitive information, while malicious servers may mount adversarial attacks such as Byzantine manipulation. These vulnerabilities highlight the need to address differential privacy (DP) and Byzantine robustness within a unified framework. Existing approaches, however, often rely on unrealistic assumptions such as bounded gradients, require auxiliary server-side datasets, or fail to provide convergence guarantees. We address these limitations by proposing Byz-Clip21-SGD2M, a new algorithm that integrates robust aggregation with double momentum and carefully designed clipping. We prove high-probability convergence guarantees under standard $L$-smoothness and $σ$-sub-Gaussian gradient noise assumptions, thereby relaxing conditions that dominate prior work. Our analysis recovers state-of-the-art convergence rates in the absence of adversaries and improves utility guarantees under Byzantine and DP settings. Empirical evaluations on CNN and MLP models trained on MNIST further validate the effectiveness of our approach.

### [323] [Estimating Flow Velocity and Vehicle Angle-of-Attack from Non-invasive Piezoelectric Structural Measurements Using Deep Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.23496)
*Chandler B. Smith,S. Hales Swift,Andrew Steyer,Ihab El-Kady*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: A non-intrusive method using a dense interior piezoelectric sensor array and CNN to infer freestream velocity and AoA from structural vibrations, validated in Sandia hypersonic wind tunnel (Mach 5/8) with constant and varying tunnel operation; after short-window post-processing, achieves a mean velocity error <2.27 m/s (0.21%) and AoA error of 0.44° (8.25%) on held-out data from the same campaign.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Accurately estimate aerodynamic state variables (velocity and AoA) without direct flow instrumentation, enabling improved load prediction, flight control, and model validation through vibration-based sensing.

Method: Deploy a dense piezoelectric sensor array on the vehicle interior to capture vibrations from turbulent boundary layer pressure fluctuations. Train a convolutional neural network to invert these structural responses into velocity and AoA. Use 16 wind tunnel runs with a temporally centered held-out interval per run to form training/validation/test sets and assess intra-run temporal generalization. Apply a short-window moving-median post-processing to reduce variance under continuously varying tunnel conditions.

Result: Post-processed CNN predictions yield a mean velocity error relative to a low-pass reference velocity below 2.27 m/s (0.21%) and a mean AoA error of 0.44° (8.25%) on held-out test data within the campaign, demonstrating feasibility of vibration-based velocity and AoA estimation in a controlled lab environment.

Conclusion: Demonstrates feasibility of vibration-based, non-intrusive aerodynamic state estimation in a controlled setting, with good intra-run generalization after appropriate post-processing; further work needed to address cross-campaign generalization and flight-like conditions.

Abstract: Accurate estimation of aerodynamic state variables such as freestream velocity and angle of attack (AoA) is important for aerodynamic load prediction, flight control, and model validation. This work presents a non-intrusive method for estimating vehicle velocity and AoA from structural vibration measurements rather than direct flow instrumentation such as pitot tubes. A dense array of piezoelectric sensors mounted on the interior skin of an aeroshell capture vibrations induced by turbulent boundary layer pressure fluctuations, and a convolutional neural network (CNN) is trained to invert these structural responses to recover velocity and AoA.
  Proof-of-concept is demonstrated through controlled experiments in Sandia's hypersonic wind tunnel spanning zero and nonzero AoA configurations, Mach~5 and Mach~8 conditions, and both constant and continuously varying tunnel operations. The CNN is trained and evaluated using data from 16 wind tunnel runs, with a temporally centered held-out interval within each run used to form training, validation, and test datasets and assess intra-run temporal generalization. Raw CNN predictions exhibit increased variance during continuously varying conditions; a short-window moving-median post-processing step suppresses this variance and improves robustness. After post-processing, the method achieves a mean velocity error relative to the low-pass filtered reference velocity below 2.27~m/s (0.21\%) and a mean AoA error of $0.44^{\circ} (8.25\%)$ on held-out test data from the same experimental campaign, demonstrating feasibility of vibration-based velocity and AoA estimation in a controlled laboratory environment.

</details>
